  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3557274965" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3557274965" />
    <a title="Bryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76188905-bryan"><img alt="Bryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588030220p2/76188905.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3557274965?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan" href="/user/show/76188905-bryan">Bryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3557274965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6228974696715005408">TLDR: I am pleased to say that Shield of Thunder was a dramatic improvement from its predecessor in every way. While still maintaining the same writing style present in Lord of the Silver Bow, Gemmell has made observable improvements in his character work,  coupled together with high stakes tension and a sprinkle of political intrigue which gives weight to what would appear to be a small stakes conflict. While there were a few pacing issues and questionable narrative decisions leading up to the </span>
  <span id="freeText6228974696715005408" style="display:none">TLDR: I am pleased to say that Shield of Thunder was a dramatic improvement from its predecessor in every way. While still maintaining the same writing style present in Lord of the Silver Bow, Gemmell has made observable improvements in his character work,  coupled together with high stakes tension and a sprinkle of political intrigue which gives weight to what would appear to be a small stakes conflict. While there were a few pacing issues and questionable narrative decisions leading up to the end of Part 3, the book is saved by the dialogue between characters that make you feel a variety of emotions which indicates the mastery in Gemmell's writing to create such organic relationships between fictional--but fully realized--characters. An outstanding follow up to the original novel and a high bar for the final book in the series. <br /><br />I was never really able to finish my review of the first novel of the entry. I will try to get back to it eventually, but there was so much to discuss about that book (and life) that I had put it off for the time being. I will get back to it eventually and will publish my review once it's ready. The Lord of the Silver bow had the burden of establishing a familiar world to readers of the Classics, but with a Gemmelian twist--or reimagining. This series is my first entry into Gemmell's work, which has often been cited as inspirations for authors like John Gwynne (an author I am excited to start reading from). From what I read of the first book, there was a lot that he did right that I found was interesting and unique from other authors I have read this far. <br /><br />One of the issues I had with the first book was really the author's portrayal of the classic heroes--Helikaeon (or Aeneas), in particular. He really feels like a Gary Stu, a character without any real flaws and is perfect in every way. I understand that characters like Hektor, Aeneas and Achilles were larger than life--Gods among men; however, what worked for classical tales of heroism does not translate well to the modern novel in the contemporary age. We live in a world that celebrates comic book superheroes for a variety of reasons. We've abandoned the original portrayal of Golden Era Superman to a grittier and edgier Man of Steel because we are able to relate more to his struggles and flaws. Helikaeon was Golden Era Superman, nicknamed after the Apollo himself. <br />So much so that I feel that, realizing this, Gemmell's first priority was to mortally wound Helikaeon behind the scenes, having him sit out for the majority of the second book. <br /><br />This decision was better off for it. I read reviews from people who were dissatisfied with following the new characters: Kalliades, Banokles and Piria for the majority of this novel and, while I do understand the feeling of not being able to follow one's favorite characters from the previous novel, I fall on the other side of this complaint that celebrates these newer characters. On one hand, I loved their banter (which was teased in the previous book leading up to the Final Conflict) which only highlights their relationship with one another while, on the other hand, really appreciated Gemmell's willingness to add a lot of depth to each character so that we learn about who they are, enriching the progression of their relationship with one another as we read through the novel. <br /><br />"You are an intelligent man. You know what should have happened... We are so alike, Kalliades. Closed off from life, no friends...loved ones. That is why we need Banokles. He is life, rich and raw, in all its glory. No subtlety, no guile. He is the fire we gather around, and his light pushes back the shadows we fear." <br /><br />Kalliades, by far, was the best written character in this book and Gemmell really took the time to flesh him out much more than he did for Andromache, from whom the book had garnered its title. In the manner he is depicted, I had often imagined him to look the part of Brad Pitt's Achilles or his cousin Patroclus. He's a competent fighter, but his true strengths lie with his guile and strategic mind. There was always a mystery to the man that, once uncovered, made him so much more layered and more vulnerable as a result. Kalliades was a man and nowhere near the same God-tier level as Hektor, Achilles or even Helikaeon which made him much more intriguing to follow--he felt mortal, 3-dimensional and more down to Earth than the previously mentioned heroes who are more one note in nature (in my opinion). **Minor spoiler: For example, there was an argument between he and Hektor in the 3rd act of the novel that really shows the character of each. Where Hektor was the Paragon of Goodness and Nobility (pretty standard one note good guy that everyone loves), Kalliades' position felt more "real".<br /><br />Again, I cannot emphasize enough that Gemell does an amazing job here, characterizing each of these characters, fleshing out their relationships and creating an amazing amount of tension even with such small stakes. Other highlights included Odysseus and Andromache, but they were overshadowed (in my opinion) by Kalliades. My boy Ganny (the pig) even tugged at my heartstrings. <br /><br />Let me demonstrate an example of how Gemmell is able to pull off both an intriguing conflict and a development of a friendship with a small scale event. There was to be a fist fight between Banokles and Leukon, a sailor who was renowned to be the best fistfighter in Odysseus's crew, which determined who would be the champion representing Ithaca in Hektor's wedding games. We know Banokles to be a capable and stalwart warrior on his own rights, but there was always that feeling of apprehension when it came to his chances of besting who was known to have been one of the toughest fighters across the many lands. To make things even spicier, there was to be a wager--a wager that I, myself, was hesitant to make--between Kalliades and Idomeneos. The Legendary Sword of Argurios for some shabby breastplate. Yikes. Then Gemmell slapped me with one of the most memorable quotes from this book that really encompasses Kalliades's character and the meaning of friendship: <br /><br />"Yes, I treasure the sword of Argurios, but it is still just a sword. Banokles is a friend. There is not enough gold in all the world to buy such a friendship." <br /><br />I really enjoyed this book once I was able to have a more consistent reading schedule. The book is still plagued with issues I've had from the first book; for example, the nonsensical time skips within the same paragraph as characters both experience flashbacks to when they were younger AND even a day/week/month/year jump forward in time. It's egregious! I also found the events leading up to and, even, at the start of the Third Act sloppily done. It was as if Gemmell was in a hurry to finish everything and then time skip into the future, not allowing the reader to marinate the feelings that each character might have gotten through. I guess it can be argued that the author trusts the reader enough to infer from context in the later sections of the novel, but I am honestly not a fan and would have liked to see it played through. In addition, while there are pacing issues littered throughout the novel here and there, these complaints do not detract from my overall enjoyment of the novel and my impressions on the many improvements Gemmell had made in Shield of Thunder since the debut. I highly recommend giving this book a go, followed up by the last book in the series--his final book of his legacy: The Fall of Kings.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6228974696715005408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Conn Iggulden is just such a writer, bringing to vivid life one of the most fascinating eras in human history. In a true masterpiece of historical fiction, Iggulden takes us on a breathtaking journey through ancient Rome, sweeping us into a realm of tyrants and slaves, of dark intrigues and seething passions. What emerges is both a grand romantic tale of coming-of-age in the Roman Empire and a vibrant portrait of the early years of a man who would become the most powerful ruler on earth: Julius Caesar. On the lush Italian peninsula, a new empire is taking shape. At its heart is the city of Rome, a place of glory and decadence, beauty and bloodshed. Against this vivid backdrop, two boys are growing to manhood, dreaming of battles, fame, and glory in service of the mightiest empire the world has ever known. One is the son of a senator, a boy of privilege and ambition to whom much has been given and from whom much is expected. The other is a bastard child, a boy of strength and cunning, whose love for his adoptive family-and his adoptive brother-will be the most powerful force in his life. As young Gaius and Marcus are trained in the art of combat-under the tutelage of one of Rome\'s most fearsome gladiators-Rome itself is being rocked by the art of treachery and ambition, caught in a tug-of-war as two rival generals, Marius and Sulla, push the empire toward civil war. For Marcus, a bloody campaign in Greece will become a young soldier\'s proving ground. For Gaius, the equally deadly infighting of the Roman Senate will be the battlefield where he hones his courage and skill. And for both, the love of an extraordinary slave girl will be an honor each will covet but only one will win. The two friends are forced to walk different paths, and by the time they meet again everything will have changed. Both will have known love, loss, and violence. And the land where they were once innocent will be thrust into the grip of bitter conflict-a conflict that will set Roman against Roman...and put their friendship to the ultimate test. Brilliantly interweaving history and adventure, Conn Iggulden conjures a stunning array of contrasts-from the bloody stench of a battlefield to the opulence of the greatest city in history, from the tenderness of a lover to the treachery of an assassin. Superbly rendered, grippingly told, Emperor, The Gates of Rome is a work of vaulting imagination from a powerful new voice in historical fiction. &quot;From the Hardcover edition.&quot;<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11567211420735136124">David Andrew Gemmell was a bestselling British author of heroic fantasy. A former journalist and newspaper editor, Gemmell had his first work of fiction published in 1984. He went on to write over thirty novels. Best known for his debut, Legend, Gemmell's works display violence, yet also explores themes in honour, loyalty and redemption. With over one million copies sold, his work continues to sel</span>
  <span id="freeText11567211420735136124" style="display:none">David Andrew Gemmell was a bestselling British author of heroic fantasy. A former journalist and newspaper editor, Gemmell had his first work of fiction published in 1984. He went on to write over thirty novels. Best known for his debut, Legend, Gemmell's works display violence, yet also explores themes in honour, loyalty and redemption. With over one million copies sold, his work continues to sell worldwide.</span>
